The nominal form confirms CHAR-6's quality-character: innocence is a condition the person possesses (or fails to possess), not a direction or act. The semantic range of niq.qa.von is narrow within this corpus: it specifically names the moral cleanness that enables approach to God and protects against guilt-shame. It does not range into the social-status vocabulary of shame's other terms.

---

---

##### T7.1.4 — Does the vocabulary include terms that distinguish distinct aspects of this characteristic — disposition versus act, received versus given, condition versus quality?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Yes. The distinction between *bosh* (the felt experience of shame — more subjective, inner) and *ke.lim.mah* (the disgrace as witnessed and socially visible — more objective, outward) maps onto a genuine inner-outer distinction within CHAR-1. *Cha.pher* (disappointed, confounded) carries the cognitive-collapse dimension (the failing of a confident expectation). *Bu.shah* (shame as covering, enveloping condition) captures the sustained-burden dimension. *Aischunē* names the condition; *aischunō* the verbal action of being ashamed; *kataischunō* the thoroughly-shamed state. The vocabulary thus distinguishes inner subjective experience, social visibility, cognitive collapse, chronic burden, and the act vs. condition of shame.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

Ka.lam operates primarily as a verb (being humiliated). Sha.phel operates as verb (Hiphil — causing to be brought low) and adjective (sha.phel nefesh — lowly of spirit). The verbal dominance confirms CHAR-2's action-character: it is something done, not merely something possessed. Semantic range covers bodily-social, national-political, and cosmic dimensions (moon and sun ashamed, Isa 24:23) — CHAR-2 applies wherever pride-structures or elevated standing exists.

---

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary terms: Atimazō (G0818): to dishonour, treat as without honour — active verb, the dishonoring act. Atimia (G0819): dishonour as a state — the condition of assigned or received diminished worth. Entrepō (G1788) in its absence register: the failure of the regard-faculty. Paradeigmatizō (G3856): to expose publicly, hold up to contempt. Roots: a-timē — without honour, without worth — the alpha-privative of honour. Structural opposite: timaō (to honour, to assign worth) and timē (honour as assigned worth-recognition). The evidence pairs these explicitly in Joh 8:49. The CHAR-3 corpus is entirely NT — the OT vocabulary for relational worth-denial (qa.lah, H7034; entrepō in its absence register) is thinner. The NT's christological context gives CHAR-3 its sharpest definition: dishonour as what is directed at Jesus personally and at God through human hypocrisy. Person-type: the "unjust judge" (Luk 18:2) is the closest person-type but is not a technical term. Supplication: the widow's persistent coming to the judge (Luk 18:3) is a supplication for the reversal of CHAR-3 — the only supplication-context in the corpus.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Aischros is consistently adjectival — it modifies conduct, gain, or situations. Aschēmosunē is consistently nominal — naming the quality. The adjectival/nominal dominance confirms CHAR-4's quality-character: it is about the property of things (shameful conduct, indecent acts) rather than the experience of persons. The semantic range is limited: all primary CHAR-4 terms specifically name the quality of moral shamefulness — they do not range into the social-status register of the shame vocabulary.

---

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

Exoutheneō operates as a verb (to despise, to treat as nothing). Atimazō operates as a verb (to dishonour, to treat shamefully). Loidoria is a noun (reviling, verbal abuse). The verbal dominance confirms CHAR-5's action-character: the contempt is always something done — an active verbal or physical or attitudinal act. The semantic range spans inner attitude (VCG-01 — the exoutheneō of the self-righteous), physical violence (VCG-02 — the atimazō of violent treatment), and verbal assault (VCG-03 — the loidoria of reputation-attack).

---

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

Both terms function primarily as nouns naming a condition: "in the innocence of my hands" (Gen 20:5; Psa 26:6) — the innocence is a quality of the hands named nominally. The nominal form confirms CHAR-6's quality-character: innocence is a condition the person possesses (or fails to possess), not a direction or act. The semantic range of niq.qa.von is narrow within this corpus: it specifically names the moral cleanness that enables approach to God and protects against guilt-shame. It does not range into the social-status vocabulary of shame's other terms.

---

---

##### T7.1.5 — Does the vocabulary include a term for the structural opposite or absence of this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Yes. The structural opposite of shame is named through honour-vocabulary: *kabod* (glory/honour), *timē* (honour), *doxa* (glory) — these are not in the CHAR-1 corpus itself but appear in the contrast structures ("instead of your shame there shall be a double portion," Isa 61:7; "enemies clothed with shame but his crown shines," Psa 132:18). The term for the absence of shame is evidenced in the negative constructions: "you will not be ashamed" (future promise), "were not ashamed" (Gen 2:25), "will not be put to shame" (Psa 22:5 et al.) — the negated verb serves as the structural opposite vocabulary.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

Rum (exalt, lift up) and nasa (lift) are the structural opposites named in the evidence: "he brings low and he exalts" (1Sa 2:7); "putting down one and lifting up another" (Psa 75:7). Kabod (glory, honor) is the state CHAR-2 replaces (Hos 4:7 — "change their glory into shame"; Hab 2:16 — "shame instead of glory").

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary terms: Atimazō (G0818): to dishonour, treat as without honour — active verb, the dishonoring act. Atimia (G0819): dishonour as a state — the condition of assigned or received diminished worth. Entrepō (G1788) in its absence register: the failure of the regard-faculty. Paradeigmatizō (G3856): to expose publicly, hold up to contempt. Roots: a-timē — without honour, without worth — the alpha-privative of honour. Structural opposite: timaō (to honour, to assign worth) and timē (honour as assigned worth-recognition). The evidence pairs these explicitly in Joh 8:49. The CHAR-3 corpus is entirely NT — the OT vocabulary for relational worth-denial (qa.lah, H7034; entrepō in its absence register) is thinner. The NT's christological context gives CHAR-3 its sharpest definition: dishonour as what is directed at Jesus personally and at God through human hypocrisy. Person-type: the "unjust judge" (Luk 18:2) is the closest person-type but is not a technical term. Supplication: the widow's persistent coming to the judge (Luk 18:3) is a supplication for the reversal of CHAR-3 — the only supplication-context in the corpus.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Kalos (good, beautiful, proper) and euschēmosunē (decency, propriety, proper form — the opposite of aschēmosunē) are the structural opposites. The evidence implies these opposites: "by the open statement of the truth" (2Cor 4:2) vs. "shameful, underhanded ways"; "keeping his garments on" (Rev 16:15) vs. "going about naked and exposed."

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

The structural opposite of exoutheneō (treating as nothing) is acceptance — receiving the person as of genuine worth (Gal 4:14 — "received me as an angel of God"). The structural opposite of atimazō is timaō (to honour). The structural opposite of loidoria is eulogia (blessing — 1Pe 3:9: "do not repay reviling for reviling, but on the contrary bless").

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

The structural opposite of niq.qa.von (innocence/cleanness) is guilt or defilement — the inner moral condition that produces guilt-shame. The evidence names this opposite indirectly through Hos 8:5's "incapacity for innocence" — the state of having no access to the inner cleanness that innocence names.

---

##### T7.1.6 — Does the vocabulary include a person-type term — a term for the one who habitually possesses or exercises this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (S)

The CHAR-1 corpus does not include a specific person-type term naming "the one who characteristically experiences shame" in the way that "the humble" or "the proud" are person-type terms. The closest are descriptive: "a son who acts shamefully" (Pro 17:2), "the shameful" — but these name conduct rather than a person-type defined by shame as an inner characteristic. The evidence is silent on a person-type term for CHAR-1.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (S)

The evidence does not include a person-type term for the one who characteristically undergoes CHAR-2. The "proud" is the person-type who invites CHAR-2, not the person-type defined by it.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary terms: Atimazō (G0818): to dishonour, treat as without honour — active verb, the dishonoring act. Atimia (G0819): dishonour as a state — the condition of assigned or received diminished worth. Entrepō (G1788) in its absence register: the failure of the regard-faculty. Paradeigmatizō (G3856): to expose publicly, hold up to contempt. Roots: a-timē — without honour, without worth — the alpha-privative of honour. Structural opposite: timaō (to honour, to assign worth) and timē (honour as assigned worth-recognition). The evidence pairs these explicitly in Joh 8:49. The CHAR-3 corpus is entirely NT — the OT vocabulary for relational worth-denial (qa.lah, H7034; entrepō in its absence register) is thinner. The NT's christological context gives CHAR-3 its sharpest definition: dishonour as what is directed at Jesus personally and at God through human hypocrisy. Person-type: the "unjust judge" (Luk 18:2) is the closest person-type but is not a technical term. Supplication: the widow's persistent coming to the judge (Luk 18:3) is a supplication for the reversal of CHAR-3 — the only supplication-context in the corpus.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Person-type: "false teachers" (Tit 1:11) and "those who glory in their shame" (Phili 3:19) are the VCG-01 and VCG-03 person-types respectively — but these are descriptive rather than technical terms.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (S)

The CHAR-5 corpus does not include a specific person-type term for the one who characteristically receives contempt-produced shame. Bartimaeus functions as a narrative person-type (the despised beggar) but is not a technical term. Silent.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (S)

The 8-verse corpus does not include a specific person-type term for the one who characteristically possesses CHAR-6. The psalmist of Psa 26 functions as a narrative person-type (the innocent one who approaches God's altar) but is not a technical term. Silent.

---

##### T7.1.7 — Does the vocabulary include a supplication or seeking term — a term for the act of seeking this characteristic from another?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Yes, indirectly. The repeated Psalm petitions "let me not be put to shame" (Psa 25:2; 31:1; 71:1; 119:31) constitute a supplication pattern — the person seeking from God the protection from shame. The supplication is the negative request: "shield me from shame." This is not a specific lexical term but a grammatical-formulaic pattern within the bosh corpus that constitutes a distinctive mode of seeking.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (S)

Not evidenced. CHAR-2 is not typically the object of supplication in the corpus.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary terms: Atimazō (G0818): to dishonour, treat as without honour — active verb, the dishonoring act. Atimia (G0819): dishonour as a state — the condition of assigned or received diminished worth. Entrepō (G1788) in its absence register: the failure of the regard-faculty. Paradeigmatizō (G3856): to expose publicly, hold up to contempt. Roots: a-timē — without honour, without worth — the alpha-privative of honour. Structural opposite: timaō (to honour, to assign worth) and timē (honour as assigned worth-recognition). The evidence pairs these explicitly in Joh 8:49. The CHAR-3 corpus is entirely NT — the OT vocabulary for relational worth-denial (qa.lah, H7034; entrepō in its absence register) is thinner. The NT's christological context gives CHAR-3 its sharpest definition: dishonour as what is directed at Jesus personally and at God through human hypocrisy. Person-type: the "unjust judge" (Luk 18:2) is the closest person-type but is not a technical term. Supplication: the widow's persistent coming to the judge (Luk 18:3) is a supplication for the reversal of CHAR-3 — the only supplication-context in the corpus.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (S)

Supplication is not evidenced — CHAR-4 is not the object of supplication. Silent.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (S)

Not evidenced in a formal sense. Psa 14:6's "shame the plans of the poor" implies a supplication context but does not constitute a formal supplication term. Silent.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

Psa 26:6's "I wash my hands in innocence and go around your altar" constitutes a seeking term in the cultic sense: the hand-washing is the preparatory act for seeking access to God's presence at the altar. The seeking is not a verbal supplication but a ritual act of approach — and CHAR-6 is the inner condition that this approach-act requires and declares.

---

##### T7.1.8 — What does the relationship between the OT Hebrew vocabulary and the NT Greek vocabulary reveal about continuity or development of this characteristic across the Testaments?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Strong continuity. The NT Greek vocabulary (*aischunē*, *aischunō*, *kataischunō*, *entrepō*, *entropē*) functionally parallels the OT Hebrew (*bosh*, *ke.lim.mah*, *ka.lam*) without significant conceptual addition. The most notable NT development is the christological focus of shame: Paul's "I will not be ashamed" (2Cor 10:8; Phili 1:20) grounds shame-protection in apostolic authority from God and eschatological hope rather than in the general OT pattern of trust-in-God. Heb 12:2 makes Christ's own shame-endurance the model and anchor for the believer's shame-bearing. The NT does not introduce a new inner-being understanding of shame but focuses the OT evidence christologically and ecclesially (shame now operating in the community of Christ, with Christ as the primary anchor).

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

The OT ka.lam and sha.phel vocabulary is rich and extensive; the NT vocabulary for CHAR-2 is thinner. The NT deploys atimia (1Cor 15:43), fimoō (silencing), entrepō (expected respect not shown), and kataischunō (Luk 13:17) for CHAR-2 situations. The most significant NT development is 1Cor 15:43's theological deployment of atimia for the mortal body's state — placing CHAR-2 within the eschatological seed-glory framework.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary terms: Atimazō (G0818): to dishonour, treat as without honour — active verb, the dishonoring act. Atimia (G0819): dishonour as a state — the condition of assigned or received diminished worth. Entrepō (G1788) in its absence register: the failure of the regard-faculty. Paradeigmatizō (G3856): to expose publicly, hold up to contempt. Roots: a-timē — without honour, without worth — the alpha-privative of honour. Structural opposite: timaō (to honour, to assign worth) and timē (honour as assigned worth-recognition). The evidence pairs these explicitly in Joh 8:49. The CHAR-3 corpus is entirely NT — the OT vocabulary for relational worth-denial (qa.lah, H7034; entrepō in its absence register) is thinner. The NT's christological context gives CHAR-3 its sharpest definition: dishonour as what is directed at Jesus personally and at God through human hypocrisy. Person-type: the "unjust judge" (Luk 18:2) is the closest person-type but is not a technical term. Supplication: the widow's persistent coming to the judge (Luk 18:3) is a supplication for the reversal of CHAR-3 — the only supplication-context in the corpus.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

The CHAR-4 corpus is primarily NT except for the OT idolatrous-shameful-objects material (Jer 3:24; 11:13; Hos 2:5; 9:10) which uses the bo.shet vocabulary in its shameful-object register (the idol is "the shameful thing"). The NT develops the conscience-register (2Cor 4:2; Eph 5:12) and the inverted-conscience register (Phili 3:19; Hos 4:18) with greater explicitness and christological grounding than the OT. The OT's shameful-object vocabulary and the NT's conscience-vocabulary together constitute CHAR-4's full canonical arc.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

The OT evidence for CHAR-5 is thinner: the Rut 2:15 and 1Sa 25:7/15 passages (ka.lam in its protection-from-reproach register) provide the negative case (not subjecting the vulnerable to contempt-shame). The Psa 14:6 case (shaming the poor's plans) provides the positive contempt-targeting case. The NT develops CHAR-5's exoutheneō vocabulary extensively (11 of CHAR-5's 24 verses use exoutheneō). The NT also provides CHAR-5's most theologically concentrated statement: Act 4:11 (the rejected stone becoming the cornerstone). The development reveals CHAR-5's explicit christological grounding in the NT that is present but implicit in the OT.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

The CHAR-6 corpus is entirely OT and entirely Hebrew: niq.qa.von and qe.ha.von are OT vocabulary terms with no NT equivalents cited in the corpus. The NT equivalent vocabulary for the innocence-as-counter-to-shame function would include terms like katharos (clean), amomos (blameless), and anenklētos (irreproachable) — these are not in CHAR-6's corpus but would be the NT vocabulary covering similar ground. The exclusively OT character of the corpus is a significant finding: the innocence-as-shame-counter concept in CHAR-6's specific terms is an OT phenomenon; the NT develops the same concept through different vocabulary (conscience, blamelessness, etc.) and primarily in CHAR-1's M07-C register rather than in a separate CHAR-6 characteristic.

---

##### T7.1.9 — Is there a term newly coined in the NT period for this characteristic — and if so, what does that coinage reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (S)

No clearly new-coined NT term for CHAR-1 is evidenced. *Kataischunō* has stronger attestation in the NT but is not a new coinage. *Entropē* (shame in the sense of moral-awakening) is used specifically in Paul's epistolary context (1Cor 6:5; 15:34) in a way that develops the corrective-shame register, but it is not a newly coined term.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (S)

No clearly new-coined NT term for CHAR-2 evidenced.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary terms: Atimazō (G0818): to dishonour, treat as without honour — active verb, the dishonoring act. Atimia (G0819): dishonour as a state — the condition of assigned or received diminished worth. Entrepō (G1788) in its absence register: the failure of the regard-faculty. Paradeigmatizō (G3856): to expose publicly, hold up to contempt. Roots: a-timē — without honour, without worth — the alpha-privative of honour. Structural opposite: timaō (to honour, to assign worth) and timē (honour as assigned worth-recognition). The evidence pairs these explicitly in Joh 8:49. The CHAR-3 corpus is entirely NT — the OT vocabulary for relational worth-denial (qa.lah, H7034; entrepō in its absence register) is thinner. The NT's christological context gives CHAR-3 its sharpest definition: dishonour as what is directed at Jesus personally and at God through human hypocrisy. Person-type: the "unjust judge" (Luk 18:2) is the closest person-type but is not a technical term. Supplication: the widow's persistent coming to the judge (Luk 18:3) is a supplication for the reversal of CHAR-3 — the only supplication-context in the corpus.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (S)

No clearly new-coined NT term for CHAR-4 evidenced. Silent.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (S)

No clearly new-coined NT term for CHAR-5. Exoutheneō is a NT-period intensification of the contempt-vocabulary (the alpha-privative with "nothing" as root is more radical than OT equivalents), but it is not newly coined for this concept.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (S)

No NT vocabulary for CHAR-6 is evidenced in the corpus, and therefore no NT neologism is evidenced. Silent.

---

##### T7.1.10 — What does the full vocabulary arc reveal about this characteristic's complete semantic range?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

The full vocabulary arc reveals CHAR-1 as a characteristic deeply embedded in both Testaments' inner-being vocabulary, primarily through the *bosh* family in Hebrew and the *aischun-* family in Greek. The arc shows the characteristic's stability across testamental contexts: shame is shame — the inner collapse of dignity before exposure, failure, or moral inadequacy — in both Testaments, and the vocabulary confirms this without significant discontinuity. The NT's christological focus (Heb 12:2; 2Cor 10:8; Phili 1:20) represents the most significant development: shame is now specifically engaged with the believer's relation to Christ and his cross.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

The vocabulary arc runs from the OT's concrete social-bodily humiliation (ka.lam — shaved beards, covered heads) through wisdom tradition's moral-consequence pairing (pride -> qa.lon) through prophetic Day-of-the-Lord cosmic bringing-low (sha.phel in the Isa 2 series) to the NT's eschatological frame (atimia of mortal existence -> resurrection glory). The arc reveals CHAR-2 as operating at every level of human existence — bodily, social, national, cosmic — with its final resolution eschatological.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary terms: Atimazō (G0818): to dishonour, treat as without honour — active verb, the dishonoring act. Atimia (G0819): dishonour as a state — the condition of assigned or received diminished worth. Entrepō (G1788) in its absence register: the failure of the regard-faculty. Paradeigmatizō (G3856): to expose publicly, hold up to contempt. Roots: a-timē — without honour, without worth — the alpha-privative of honour. Structural opposite: timaō (to honour, to assign worth) and timē (honour as assigned worth-recognition). The evidence pairs these explicitly in Joh 8:49. The CHAR-3 corpus is entirely NT — the OT vocabulary for relational worth-denial (qa.lah, H7034; entrepō in its absence register) is thinner. The NT's christological context gives CHAR-3 its sharpest definition: dishonour as what is directed at Jesus personally and at God through human hypocrisy. Person-type: the "unjust judge" (Luk 18:2) is the closest person-type but is not a technical term. Supplication: the widow's persistent coming to the judge (Luk 18:3) is a supplication for the reversal of CHAR-3 — the only supplication-context in the corpus.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

The vocabulary arc runs from the OT's shameful-object/idolatry register (bo.shet naming the idol as "the shameful thing" in Jer 3:24; 11:13) through the NT's conscience-evaluative register (aischros, aschēmosunē in 1Cor, Eph, Tit) to the inverted-conscience register (aischunē as gloried-in shame in Phili 3:19). The arc reveals CHAR-4 as operating across both Testaments in morally consistent terms: the evaluative standard remains God's holiness throughout; the corruption of the evaluative faculty takes different forms but is consistently identified as dangerous to the person's eschatological standing.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

The vocabulary arc moves from the OT's ka.lam in its protection-from-reproach register (Rut 2:15; 1Sa 25:7/15) through the NT's exoutheneō-dominated contempt-vocabulary (VCG-01) to the christological-reversal language (Act 4:11; 1Cor 1:28). The arc reveals CHAR-5 as a characteristic whose OT evidence is primarily protective (shielding from contempt) and whose NT evidence is primarily christological (Jesus receiving contempt, Jesus reversing contempt-verdicts). The trajectory is from protection toward transformation.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

The vocabulary arc for CHAR-6 is compact and exclusively OT: four verse-records, two terms (niq.qa.von and qe.ha.von), appearing in narrative (Gen 20:5), worship-psalm (Psa 26:6), lament-psalm (Psa 73:13), and prophecy (Hos 8:5). The arc reveals CHAR-6 as a characteristic defined by its OT cultic-covenant context: the inner moral innocence that enables approach to God in the worship context. The arc's movement from the functioning-innocence-enabling-approach (Gen 20:5; Psa 26:6) through the questioned-innocence-under-suffering (Psa 73:13) to the incapacity-for-innocence (Hos 8:5) reveals the full range of CHAR-6's operation: from its positive functioning through its testing to its loss.

---

#### T7.2 — Verse and Literary Interpretation

##### T7.2.1 — What is the function of this characteristic's primary term within its primary verse — what role does it play in the sentence and argument?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Taking Psa 44:15 (vc=37310) as a representative primary verse: "all day long my disgrace is before me, and shame has covered my face" — *bo.shet* functions as the subject of "has covered my face" — shame is the agent that covers. This is a significant grammatical observation: shame is not merely a quality the person has; it is described as a covering-agent acting on the person. The person is covered by shame; the relationship is one of passive reception. This grammatical function (shame as agent, person as recipient) confirms the condition-character of CHAR-1 across its primary vocabulary.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

Taking 2Sa 10:5 (vc=38517) for VCG-01: ka.lam functions as a stative verb ("the men were greatly ashamed/humiliated") — the men are in a state produced by what was done to them. The stative confirms CHAR-2's received-condition character: shame here is what they are in, not what they do.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary literary forms: parable (Luk 18:2-4 — the unjust judge); epistle (Rom 2:23; 2Cor 6:8; Jam 2:6; 1Cor 11:4-5); narrative (Joh 8:49). The parable form (Luk 18) uses the character of the dishonoring judge as a contrast-figure — his absent-regard makes the point precisely by its absence. Primary anchor: Joh 8:49 (vc=35000) — "I honor my Father, and you dishonor me" — the most compressed, relational, and personally directed expression of CHAR-3 in the corpus, revealing worth-denial as a relational act directed at a specific person in a specific exchange. This verse uniquely reveals worth-denial as a personal relational act within a face-to-face exchange, where the standard (honouring the Father) and the violation (dishonoring the Son) are simultaneously named.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Taking Eph 5:12 (vc=34985) as the primary anchor: aischros functions as a predicate adjective ("it is shameful even to speak of the things that they do in secret") — shame is a quality attributed to both speaking about and doing certain deeds. The predicate use confirms CHAR-4's quality-character: shamefulness is being predicated of conduct, not attributed to a person's experience.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

Taking Luk 18:9 (vc=54639) as the primary VCG-01 anchor: exoutheneō functions as a participial verb in a relative clause ("who treated others with contempt") — it characterises the inner habit of those who trust in themselves. The participial form confirms CHAR-5's dispositional character in VCG-01: contempt-producing-shame is not an isolated act but the expression of a habituated inner disposition.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

Taking Gen 20:5 (vc=22817) as the primary anchor: niq.qa.von functions as a noun in a prepositional phrase ("in the innocence of my hands") — innocence is the medium or condition in which the hands acted. The prepositional construction ("in the innocence") confirms CHAR-6's condition-character: the hands acted within the condition of innocence, meaning the moral condition enveloped and characterised the action. No guilt-stain is present to contaminate the action or bar the approach.

---

##### T7.2.2 — What literary form carries the primary verse evidence (narrative, psalm, wisdom, prophecy, epistle, apocalyptic) — and what does that form require for responsible interpretation?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

The majority of the CHAR-1 corpus is in three forms: (1) **Psalm/lament** (M07-A-VCG-01; M07-C-VCG-02): the individual or communal cry in distress, the plea for shame-removal, the imprecation of shame on enemies. The lament form requires the reader to hear shame as genuinely experienced distress, not as theological abstraction. (2) **Prophecy** (M07-B-VCG-02; M07-C-VCG-01): divine pronouncements of shame-to-come or shame-commanded. The prophetic form requires reading shame as both historically grounded and theologically meaningful. (3) **Epistle** (M07-B-VCG-05; M07-C-VCG-02): Paul's corrective use of shame and his own shame-freedom statements. The epistolary form situates shame within ongoing community formation.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

Three primary forms: (1) Narrative (2Sa 10:5; 2Ch 32:21): historical accounts of social/military humiliation. (2) Prophecy (Isa 2, 13; Jer 48-50; Nah 3:5): divine announcements of coming humiliation on nations and the proud. (3) Wisdom (Pro 11:2): the moral principle connecting pride and disgrace. Each form requires appropriate reading: narrative demands historical grounding; prophecy demands theological weight; wisdom demands recognition of the moral-law character of the pride -> disgrace connection.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary literary forms: parable (Luk 18:2-4 — the unjust judge); epistle (Rom 2:23; 2Cor 6:8; Jam 2:6; 1Cor 11:4-5); narrative (Joh 8:49). The parable form (Luk 18) uses the character of the dishonoring judge as a contrast-figure — his absent-regard makes the point precisely by its absence. Primary anchor: Joh 8:49 (vc=35000) — "I honor my Father, and you dishonor me" — the most compressed, relational, and personally directed expression of CHAR-3 in the corpus, revealing worth-denial as a relational act directed at a specific person in a specific exchange. This verse uniquely reveals worth-denial as a personal relational act within a face-to-face exchange, where the standard (honouring the Father) and the violation (dishonoring the Son) are simultaneously named.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Primary forms: epistle (2Cor 4:2; Eph 5:12; Phili 3:19; Tit 1:11; 1Cor 11:6; 14:35; Rom 1:24-27); prophecy (Jer 3:24; 11:13; Hos 2:5; 9:10); apocalyptic (Rev 16:15). The epistolary form is most prominent — Paul's letters are the primary vehicle for CHAR-4's articulated moral-evaluative judgments.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

Primary forms: parable (Luk 18:9 — the self-righteous one who despises others; Luk 20:11 — the parable of the tenants); narrative (Mar 10:50 — Bartimaeus; Luk 23:11 — the mocking of Jesus; Act 4:11 — the cornerstone); epistle (Rom 14:3/10; 1Cor 1:28; 1Cor 16:11; Gal 4:14; 1Th 5:20; 1Pe 3:9; 2Pe 2:16); OT narrative (Rut 2:15; 1Sa 25:7/15). The narrative form is most frequent in VCG-02 (concrete events of contemptuous violence). The epistolary form dominates VCG-01 (community-directed commands against internal contempt).

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

The 8-verse corpus spans four literary forms: narrative (Gen 20:5 — Abimelech's dialogue with God), worship-psalm (Psa 26:6 — liturgical approach to the altar), lament-psalm (Psa 73:13 — existential questioning of innocence's value), and prophecy (Hos 8:5 — divine indictment of incapacity for innocence). Each form requires appropriate reading: narrative demands attention to the historical interaction and divine confirmation; the worship-psalm requires recognition of the cultic-liturgical context; the lament-psalm requires holding the existential crisis alongside the broader resolution; the prophecy requires recognition of the divine indictment's weight.

---

##### T7.2.3 — What is the logical structure of key arguments in the verse evidence — what are the premises and conclusions?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Several logical structures recur. (1) **Trust → protection from shame** (Psa 22:5; 31:1; Rom 5:5; 9:33; 10:11): the argument form is: *because one trusts/believes, shame will not overtake*. The logical structure is conditional: trust is the condition; shame-protection is the consequence. (2) **Sin → shame as judgment** (M07-B-VCG-03: Pro 3:35; 6:33; Jer 2:26): the argument form is: *because one has sinned/acted foolishly, shame follows*. The logical structure is moral-consequence: failure is the antecedent; disgrace is the consequent. (3) **Shame → gateway to knowledge** (Eze 43:10-11): the argument form is: *if Israel is ashamed, then the design will be disclosed*. The logical structure is conditional gateway: shame is the necessary condition for the disclosure.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

Dominant argument form in VCG-03: if pride (inner disposition of self-exaltation), then disgrace (enforced downward movement) — this is divine law. Pro 11:2 is the compressed form. The Isa 2 series expands it: the Lord's day targets all proud and lofty, bringing them low, so the Lord alone is exalted. Logical structure: moral necessity — the proud over-reach of standing inevitably attracts the corrective action of the one who alone has absolute standing.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary literary forms: parable (Luk 18:2-4 — the unjust judge); epistle (Rom 2:23; 2Cor 6:8; Jam 2:6; 1Cor 11:4-5); narrative (Joh 8:49). The parable form (Luk 18) uses the character of the dishonoring judge as a contrast-figure — his absent-regard makes the point precisely by its absence. Primary anchor: Joh 8:49 (vc=35000) — "I honor my Father, and you dishonor me" — the most compressed, relational, and personally directed expression of CHAR-3 in the corpus, revealing worth-denial as a relational act directed at a specific person in a specific exchange. This verse uniquely reveals worth-denial as a personal relational act within a face-to-face exchange, where the standard (honouring the Father) and the violation (dishonoring the Son) are simultaneously named.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Dominant argument form: if conduct violates the moral standard (as confirmed by God's sight or created order), then it is shameful — and its shamefulness is itself a reason for renouncing it. 2Cor 4:2's argument: we renounce disgraceful ways -> we commend ourselves to everyone's conscience in the sight of God. The logic is moral-calibration: shameful = cannot bear God's sight -> therefore renounce.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

The dominant argument form in VCG-01: if one despises a fellow believer, one despises one whom God has welcomed (Rom 14:3 — "God has welcomed him"). The logic is worth-grounding in divine acceptance: the worth that contempt denies is the worth God has already assigned. For we will all stand before the judgment seat of God (Rom 14:10) — the eschatological accountability reverses the contempt-er's assumption of superiority.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

The dominant argument form: if innocence is present (heart-integrity + hand-cleanness), then approach to God is appropriate and enabled. Gen 20:5-6: Abimelech claims innocence -> God confirms it. Psa 26:6: innocence claimed through hand-washing -> approach to altar enabled. The inverse argument: if innocence-capacity is absent (Hos 8:5), then approach to God is blocked. The logic is moral prerequisite: innocence is the condition that the covenant relationship with God requires of the person.

---

##### T7.2.4 — What contextual setting carries the primary verse evidence (judicial, liturgical, covenantal, communal, eschatological) — and what does that setting reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Multiple settings. (1) **Liturgical** (Psalms, Ezr 9, Dan 9): shame expressed in the context of worship, confession, and prayer. (2) **Covenantal** (Eze 16; 36; 43; Jer 31): shame and shame-removal within the framework of God's covenant relationship with Israel. (3) **Judicial** (M07-B enemy-shame material; Isa prophecy): shame as divine judicial verdict. (4) **Communal** (Paul's letters; 2Th 3; 1Pe 3): shame operating within the Christian community as a relational instrument. (5) **Eschatological** (Isa 54:4; 61:7; Zep 3:19; Rev 3:18): shame in the context of final resolution and transformation.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

(1) Military/political (VCG-02): the battlefield and the conquered city. (2) Covenantal-prophetic (VCG-03): God's covenant relationship with his people and with the nations. (3) Community/social (VCG-01): diplomatic, royal, and cultic community contexts. (4) Eschatological (VCG-03 Day-of-the-Lord; 1Cor 15:43): the final resolution of all standing before God.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary literary forms: parable (Luk 18:2-4 — the unjust judge); epistle (Rom 2:23; 2Cor 6:8; Jam 2:6; 1Cor 11:4-5); narrative (Joh 8:49). The parable form (Luk 18) uses the character of the dishonoring judge as a contrast-figure — his absent-regard makes the point precisely by its absence. Primary anchor: Joh 8:49 (vc=35000) — "I honor my Father, and you dishonor me" — the most compressed, relational, and personally directed expression of CHAR-3 in the corpus, revealing worth-denial as a relational act directed at a specific person in a specific exchange. This verse uniquely reveals worth-denial as a personal relational act within a face-to-face exchange, where the standard (honouring the Father) and the violation (dishonoring the Son) are simultaneously named.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Primary contexts: (1) Epistolary community-formation (1Cor, 2Cor, Eph, Tit): the shameful assessed and named within the context of community instruction and moral exhortation. (2) Prophetic-covenantal (Jer, Hos): the shameful as the idolatrous objects and practices that violate Israel's covenant. (3) Apocalyptic-eschatological (Rev 16:15): the shameful as moral-spiritual unpreparedness exposed at Christ's coming.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

Primary contexts: (1) Community/relational (VCG-01: the believer-community's internal contempt practices — food disputes, ministry-status differences). (2) Passion/christological (VCG-02: the violent contempt directed at Jesus and his representatives). (3) Verbal/epistolary (VCG-03: the apostolic ministry's exposure to verbal contempt and the appropriate response). (4) Eschatological-reversal (Act 4:11; 1Cor 1:28): the final overturning of the world's contempt-verdicts.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

Primary contexts: (1) Covenant-narrative (Gen 20:5): the historical interaction between Abimelech and Abraham/God in which innocence is the decisive moral category for divine intervention. (2) Cultic-liturgical (Psa 26:6): the approach to God's altar in worship. (3) Wisdom-lament (Psa 73:13): the existential questioning of moral integrity's value under suffering. (4) Covenant-prophetic (Hos 8:5): the divine indictment of a people who have lost the capacity for the covenant moral condition.

---

##### T7.2.5 — Does any verse function as the primary anchor for this characteristic — the verse that most fully and directly expresses its essential character?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Several candidate anchors, with different cases to make for each. (1) **Ezr 9:6** (vc=65495) — "I am ashamed and blush to lift my face to you, my God, for our iniquities have risen higher than our heads" — the clearest direct expression of guilt-shame before God, the primary M07-C mode. (2) **Psa 44:15** (vc=37310) — "all day long my disgrace is before me, and shame has covered my face" — the clearest direct expression of chronic unjust-wound shame, the primary M07-A mode. (3) **Jer 8:12** (vc=65553) — "they feel no shame... they did not know how to blush" — the clearest direct evidence for the seared-conscience register and shame's moral function. (4) **Heb 12:2** (vc=34970) — "despising the shame" — the christological anchor for the voluntary-bearing mode. The case is strongest for Ezr 9:6 as the primary anchor: it captures the inner phenomenology (felt shame, bodily expression — blushing, not lifting the face), the relational direction (before God, "my God"), the moral cause (iniquities, guilt), and the covenantal context simultaneously.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

Isa 2:11 (vc=21163): "the haughty looks of man shall be brought low, and the lofty pride of men shall be humbled, and the Lord alone will be exalted in that day." This verse captures the mechanism (brought low), the target (the proud inner orientation), and the telos (God alone exalted) simultaneously. For VCG-01, 2Sa 10:5 is the primary anchor.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary literary forms: parable (Luk 18:2-4 — the unjust judge); epistle (Rom 2:23; 2Cor 6:8; Jam 2:6; 1Cor 11:4-5); narrative (Joh 8:49). The parable form (Luk 18) uses the character of the dishonoring judge as a contrast-figure — his absent-regard makes the point precisely by its absence. Primary anchor: Joh 8:49 (vc=35000) — "I honor my Father, and you dishonor me" — the most compressed, relational, and personally directed expression of CHAR-3 in the corpus, revealing worth-denial as a relational act directed at a specific person in a specific exchange. This verse uniquely reveals worth-denial as a personal relational act within a face-to-face exchange, where the standard (honouring the Father) and the violation (dishonoring the Son) are simultaneously named.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Primary anchor: Eph 5:12 (vc=34985) — "it is shameful even to speak of the things that they do in secret." This verse most fully and directly expresses CHAR-4's essential character: the threshold-principle — conduct so morally degraded that even naming it violates the conscience. The verse reveals that CHAR-4 is not merely about assessment but about a level of moral degradation that excludes the possibility of open acknowledgment. No other verse establishes this threshold-principle with the same clarity.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

Primary anchor: Act 4:11 (vc=54638) — "this Jesus is the stone that was rejected by you, the builders, which has become the cornerstone." This verse most fully and directly expresses CHAR-5's essential character: the contempt-verdict (rejected by the builders) and its divine reversal (became the cornerstone) in a single compressed statement. The verse reveals that CHAR-5 is not the final word — divine reversal is the ultimate response to contempt-produced shame.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

Primary anchor: Gen 20:5 (vc=22817/22821) — "In the integrity of my heart and the innocence of my hands I have done this." This verse most fully and directly expresses CHAR-6's essential character: the simultaneous naming of the two dimensions (heart-integrity = inner will; hand-innocence = outward action), both working together to constitute the moral condition that protects against guilt-shame and enables approach to God. No other verse in the 8-verse corpus simultaneously names both the inner and the outer dimensions of CHAR-6 with such directness and completeness.

---

##### T7.2.6 — What does the primary anchor verse reveal that no other verse reveals?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

Ezr 9:6 (vc=65495) uniquely reveals the simultaneity of shame's dimensions: the *felt* shame ("I am ashamed"), the *bodily* expression ("blush to lift my face"), the *relational direction* ("to you, my God"), the *moral cause* ("our iniquities"), and the *scale* ("risen higher than our heads, mounted up to the heavens"). No other single verse in the corpus simultaneously names all five dimensions. What Ezr 9:6 reveals beyond all others is that shame is the constitutive experience of the guilty person standing before the holy God — not one experience among many, but the defining inner state of that encounter. The impossibility of lifting the face is not merely a posture but the full inner reality of the person before God in that moment: they cannot stand before what they face.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

Isa 2:11 uniquely reveals the three-dimensional structure of CHAR-2: mechanism (haughty looks and lofty pride brought low), target (inner orientations of self-exaltation), and eschatological purpose (God alone exalted in that day). No other verse simultaneously names all three. The verse makes explicit that CHAR-2's divine-abasement mode is teleological — it serves the goal of God's proper exaltation over against all human pride-structures.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Primary literary forms: parable (Luk 18:2-4 — the unjust judge); epistle (Rom 2:23; 2Cor 6:8; Jam 2:6; 1Cor 11:4-5); narrative (Joh 8:49). The parable form (Luk 18) uses the character of the dishonoring judge as a contrast-figure — his absent-regard makes the point precisely by its absence. Primary anchor: Joh 8:49 (vc=35000) — "I honor my Father, and you dishonor me" — the most compressed, relational, and personally directed expression of CHAR-3 in the corpus, revealing worth-denial as a relational act directed at a specific person in a specific exchange. This verse uniquely reveals worth-denial as a personal relational act within a face-to-face exchange, where the standard (honouring the Father) and the violation (dishonoring the Son) are simultaneously named.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Eph 5:12 uniquely reveals that CHAR-4 has a threshold: the conscience's evaluative operation does not merely grade conduct on a scale of shameful-to-honourable but identifies certain conduct as so degraded that it cannot be named. This reveals the conscience as having a built-in censor that exceeds simple moral assessment — some conduct falls outside the range of what moral discourse can legitimately handle. No other verse in the CHAR-4 corpus establishes this threshold-quality with the same explicitness.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

Act 4:11 uniquely reveals the eschatological structure of CHAR-5: the rejected stone — the one subjected to the builders' contempt-verdict — is the very one God has made the cornerstone. No other verse simultaneously names both the contempt-rejection (the human verdict) and the divine reversal (God's counter-verdict) with such compression. The verse reveals that CHAR-5's shame is not divinely endorsed: God acts specifically against the contempt-verdict by making the rejected one the foundation.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

Gen 20:5 uniquely reveals the two-dimensional structure of CHAR-6: the inner will (integrity of heart) and the outer action (innocence of hands) together constituting the moral condition. No other verse in the corpus names both dimensions simultaneously. The verse also reveals CHAR-6's functional character as an appeal before God — it is not merely a psychological state but a claim made before the divine audience, which God then confirms (Gen 20:6). This confirms CHAR-6 as a relational moral characteristic: it is constituted in the encounter between the person's moral condition and God's assessment of it.

---

#### T7.3 — Human Science Frameworks

##### T7.3.1 — Which human science framework (psychology, moral philosophy, developmental psychology, sociology, anthropology, or other) is most useful as an interpretive lens for this characteristic?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

The science extract (wa-m07-shame-scienceextract-v1_0-20260513.md) is available and should be the primary reference for this T7.3 series. Based on the verse evidence read and the general orientation of shame research: **Psychology of shame** (particularly the affect theory and self-conscious emotion literature) is the most directly relevant framework. Shame as a self-conscious emotion — an emotion that involves the self as object, a negative evaluation of the whole self, and a drive toward concealment — parallels the biblical evidence closely (the face-covering, the inability to lift the head, the withdrawal from the public space). The **developmental/relational** framework (how shame develops in relation to significant others' evaluations) also parallels the M07-A unjust-wound material (shame inflicted by enemies and community). The science extract should be read for the specific frameworks it identifies before making the T7.3.2-3.4 findings.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

Sociology of status and social hierarchy is most directly relevant for VCG-01 and VCG-02. Social stratification theory, shame research (social humiliation dimension), and political science of military defeat and state collapse all illuminate the evidence. For VCG-03, moral philosophy (the ethics of pride and its correction) and theological anthropology (the proper ordering of human standing before God) are the relevant frameworks.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Social psychology's research on in-group/out-group worth-assignment, status dynamics, and stigma is directly relevant to CHAR-3. The sociology of recognition (Hegel-derived, Honneth) is even more directly relevant: CHAR-3 is precisely the failure of recognition — the refusal to acknowledge another's worth within the relational field. The divergence: the sociology of recognition frames worth-recognition as socially constructed; the verse evidence grounds it in divine assignment (the poor man has worth before God regardless of the community's judgment). This divergence reveals CHAR-3's theological anchor: worth is not ultimately socially assigned but divinely given, and CHAR-3 violates that divine assignment.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Moral philosophy (applied ethics, ethics of propriety and indecency) is the most directly relevant framework for CHAR-4. The concept of moral propriety — conduct that can bear scrutiny before community and moral standard — is a philosophical category (Aristotle's sense of what is aischron, base or shameful, as the object of moral recoil). Phenomenology of the conscience (Husserl, Levinas) is relevant for the conscience's recognitive operation.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

Social psychology's research on ostracism, stigma, and social exclusion is most directly relevant for CHAR-5. Kipling Williams's ostracism research (the experience of being treated as invisible or of no account) illuminates the exoutheneō vocabulary: being treated as if one does not exist produces the severest form of social pain. Erving Goffman's stigma theory illuminates how contempt-assigned identities (the beggar's cloak in Mar 10:50) become constitutive of the person's self-understanding.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

Moral philosophy's concept of moral integrity (Harry Frankfurt's concept of wholeheartedness — acting from a will that is not divided between competing desires; Aristotle's concept of moral virtue as the stable disposition to act rightly) is most directly relevant for CHAR-6. The "integrity of my heart" (Gen 20:5) is precisely what Frankfurt would call a wholehearted will: not divided between covenant faithfulness and deliberate wrongdoing. Psychology's research on clear conscience and its effects on well-being and approach behaviour is also relevant: research consistently shows that a clear conscience enables confident engagement; CHAR-6's hand-washing enabling the approach to the altar (Psa 26:6) reflects this principle.

---

##### T7.3.2 — Where the human science framework illuminates the verse evidence — making a finding more coherent or complete — what does it reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

The psychology of shame illuminates CHAR-1's constitutional anatomy: the distinction between shame (whole-self evaluation) and guilt (behaviour evaluation) maps onto the biblical distinction between unjust-wound shame (the person's inner dignity collapsed by what others do) and guilt-shame (the person's response to their own moral failure registered before God). The science framework's identification of shame's concealment-drive (the desire to hide, disappear, cover the face) illuminates why face-covering is the primary bodily expression in the verse evidence. The science framework's attention to the role of contempt in triggering shame illuminates the M06-M07 structural link (contempt as CHAR-5's source-side, shame as CHAR-1's received face).

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

The sociology of status clarifies why the shaved beard (2Sa 10:5) produces such total social incapacity: in honour-shame cultures, bodily signs of disgrace are socially constitutive — they define what social interactions are available to the bearer. The social shame literature's observation that humiliation involves the collapse of public standing illuminates the Isa 2 series' targeting of "haughty looks" — proud social displays are what CHAR-2 specifically corrects.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Social psychology's research on in-group/out-group worth-assignment, status dynamics, and stigma is directly relevant to CHAR-3. The sociology of recognition (Hegel-derived, Honneth) is even more directly relevant: CHAR-3 is precisely the failure of recognition — the refusal to acknowledge another's worth within the relational field. The divergence: the sociology of recognition frames worth-recognition as socially constructed; the verse evidence grounds it in divine assignment (the poor man has worth before God regardless of the community's judgment). This divergence reveals CHAR-3's theological anchor: worth is not ultimately socially assigned but divinely given, and CHAR-3 violates that divine assignment.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

Aristotle's analysis of aischros (the base, the shameful) as conduct that cannot bear the light of the community's moral gaze illuminates Eph 5:12's threshold-principle: the shameful is constituted precisely by its incompatibility with public moral discourse. The philosophical tradition's emphasis on the role of shame in moral regulation (the shame-culture analysis) illuminates why CHAR-4's evaluative operation is a social-moral phenomenon as well as an individual-conscience one.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

The psychology of ostracism illuminates Act 4:11's rejected stone image: research shows that being rejected and excluded produces some of the most severe psychological pain precisely because it violates the fundamental human need for belonging and worth-recognition. The verse evidence's concern with protecting the vulnerable from contempt (Rut 2:15; 1Sa 25:7/15; 1Cor 16:11) reflects an implicit understanding of contempt's psychological damage that social psychology now documents empirically.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

Frankfurt's wholeheartedness concept illuminates "integrity of my heart": the heart that is not divided between God and deliberate sin is the wholehearted will that CHAR-6 names. The psychology of clear conscience illuminates why CHAR-6 enables approach to God (Psa 26:6): research shows clear conscience enables confident approach to evaluative encounters; Psa 26:6's confident approach to the altar reflects this psychologically grounded principle in the theological context.

---

##### T7.3.3 — Where the verse evidence and the human science framework diverge, what does the divergence reveal?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (E)

The primary divergence is the theological content of CHAR-1. The human science framework treats shame as a psychological phenomenon arising from self-evaluation and social comparison — it is horizontally grounded (in relation to others' evaluations and one's own self-concept). The verse evidence adds a vertical dimension that the science framework does not access: shame before God's holiness (M07-C) is not primarily self-evaluation or social comparison but the response of the creaturely self to the divine holiness that exposes the gap between creature and creator. The M07-C mode (Ezr 9:6; Eze 36:32; 43:10) cannot be reduced to social psychology. The divergence reveals that CHAR-1 has a dimension (the shame-before-holiness) that exceeds what any horizontal framework can account for — it requires a theology of divine holiness and creaturely moral accountability.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (E)

The human science framework treats humiliation as primarily a social phenomenon generated by other humans. CHAR-2's VCG-03 material is irreducible to social dynamics — God's abasement of the proud is not the product of social competition but of divine moral judgment. The eschatological frame (1Cor 15:43) cannot be captured by any social-science framework: the mortal body's dishonor is a condition of creaturely existence that God will eschatologically transform.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Social psychology's research on in-group/out-group worth-assignment, status dynamics, and stigma is directly relevant to CHAR-3. The sociology of recognition (Hegel-derived, Honneth) is even more directly relevant: CHAR-3 is precisely the failure of recognition — the refusal to acknowledge another's worth within the relational field. The divergence: the sociology of recognition frames worth-recognition as socially constructed; the verse evidence grounds it in divine assignment (the poor man has worth before God regardless of the community's judgment). This divergence reveals CHAR-3's theological anchor: worth is not ultimately socially assigned but divinely given, and CHAR-3 violates that divine assignment.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (E)

The primary divergence: the philosophical tradition tends to ground the shameful in social convention or natural reason; CHAR-4's primary grounding is in the sight of God (2Cor 4:2) — a theological anchor that exceeds any natural-reason or convention-based framework. VCG-03's inverted conscience is a phenomenon that moral philosophy recognises (akrasia, weakness of will, moral blindness) but the scriptural diagnosis (minds set on earthly things rather than God) provides a theological depth that psychological frameworks alone cannot reach.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (E)

The primary divergence: social psychology frames contempt-produced shame as a social-psychological phenomenon whose resolution requires social re-inclusion and worth-affirmation from peers. The verse evidence grounds the reversal specifically in divine counter-assignment: God choosing the despised (1Cor 1:28), God making the rejected cornerstone (Act 4:11). The resolution does not depend on peer-acceptance but on God's authoritative worth-declaration — which may precede and override peer-contempt entirely. The divergence reveals CHAR-5's theological anchor: worth is divinely established, and divine declaration of worth overrides the contempt-system's verdict.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (E)

The primary divergence: moral philosophy and psychology ground the clear conscience in the self's own moral assessment; CHAR-6 grounds it in God's confirmation of the moral assessment (Gen 20:6 — God confirms Abimelech's innocence). The person's self-assessment is necessary but not sufficient: what makes CHAR-6 operative as a counter to guilt-shame is God's confirming verdict, not merely the person's own positive self-evaluation. The divergence reveals CHAR-6's theological anchor: moral innocence before God requires divine confirmation, not merely psychological clear-conscience.

---

##### T7.3.4 — Does the human science framework surface any aspect of this characteristic that the verse evidence has not yet addressed — and does that absence require further verse investigation?

**[CHAR-1] Shame as experienced inner state** (G)

The human science literature on shame's developmental origins (how early relational experiences shape the shame-susceptibility of the adult) is not directly addressed by the verse evidence. The Bible speaks of generational shame (Ezr 9:7; Dan 9:7-8) and the shame of early failure (Isa 54:4 — "the shame of your youth"), but does not develop a theory of how shame-susceptibility forms developmentally. Whether this is a genuine gap in the verse evidence requiring further investigation or whether the silence reflects a different framework for understanding the formation of the inner person is a question for Session D synthesis.

**[CHAR-2] Humiliation as enforced abasement** (G)

The psychology of humiliation (distinct from shame) as a social emotion may surface aspects of CHAR-2's inner phenomenology not fully addressed by the verse evidence. The psychology of humiliation's focus on the loss of social standing as a distinct emotional experience (not identical to shame-as-guilt) may warrant further verse investigation to distinguish CHAR-2's inner experience from CHAR-1's felt-shame register.

**[CHAR-3] Dishonour as relational worth-denial** (E)

Social psychology's research on in-group/out-group worth-assignment, status dynamics, and stigma is directly relevant to CHAR-3. The sociology of recognition (Hegel-derived, Honneth) is even more directly relevant: CHAR-3 is precisely the failure of recognition — the refusal to acknowledge another's worth within the relational field. The divergence: the sociology of recognition frames worth-recognition as socially constructed; the verse evidence grounds it in divine assignment (the poor man has worth before God regardless of the community's judgment). This divergence reveals CHAR-3's theological anchor: worth is not ultimately socially assigned but divinely given, and CHAR-3 violates that divine assignment.

---

**[CHAR-4] Shamefulness as moral-evaluative judgment** (G)

The psychology of moral disengagement (Bandura) — the cognitive mechanisms by which persons disengage their moral standards from their conduct — may surface aspects of the VCG-03 inverted conscience not fully addressed by the verse evidence. The verse evidence identifies the inversion but does not elaborate the psychological mechanisms by which a conscience progressively inverts its evaluative standard from God's holiness to earthly things. Whether further verse investigation is needed or whether this is a genuine silence in the evidence base remains a question for Session D synthesis.

**[CHAR-5] Shame produced by contempt and rejection** (G)

The psychology of trauma from contempt and social rejection (especially the neurological and developmental dimensions of sustained contempt's impact on the developing person) may surface aspects of Bartimaeus's accumulated shame-identity that the verse evidence does not address. The verse evidence notes the identity-deposit (beggar-cloak) and its reversal (throwing it off at Jesus's call) but does not elaborate the developmental process by which sustained contempt shapes the person's neurological and psychological self-understanding. Whether further verse investigation is needed here or whether this is a genuine silence is a question for Session D synthesis.

**[CHAR-6] Innocence as structural counter to shame** (G)

The developmental psychology of moral integrity (how moral character formation produces the capacity for clear-conscience decision-making) may surface aspects of CHAR-6's VCG-01 functioning that the 8-verse corpus does not address. The verse evidence names the functioning innocence (Gen 20:5; Psa 26:6) and its loss (Hos 8:5) but does not elaborate the formative process by which the capacity for innocence is built or sustained. Whether this is a genuine gap in the verse evidence requiring further investigation or a silence appropriate to the OT corpus's interest in the moral condition rather than its development is a question for Session D synthesis.

---
